class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Single aisle airliner family by Boeing</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For variants of this aircraft, see <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">Boeing 737 Classic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Variants\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">Boeing T-43</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Developed into</span>\n</th>\n<td><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">Boeing 737 Classic</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737 Next Generation</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a></li></ul></div>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Boeing 737</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Narrow-body_aircraft" title="Narrow-body aircraft">narrow-body aircraft</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> at its <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Renton_Factory" title="Boeing Renton Factory">Renton Factory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>.\nDeveloped to supplement the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">Boeing 727</a> on short and thin routes, the <a href="/wiki/Twinjet" title="Twinjet">twinjet</a> retains the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">707</a> fuselage cross-section and nose with two underwing <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofans</a>.  Envisioned in 1964, the initial 737-100 made its first flight in April 1967 and entered service in February 1968 with <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>.\nThe lengthened 737-200 entered service in April 1968.\nIt evolved through four generations, offering several variants for 85 to 215 passengers.\n</p><p>The 737-100/200 original variants were powered by <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_JT8D" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney JT8D">Pratt &amp; Whitney JT8D</a> low-bypass engines and offered seating for 85 to 130 passengers. Launched in 1980 and introduced in 1984, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">737 Classic</a> -300/400/500 variants were <a href="/wiki/Re-engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-engine">upgraded</a> with <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#CFM56-3B-1" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-3</a> turbofans and offered 110 to 168 seats. Introduced in 1997, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">737 Next Generation</a> (NG) -600/700/800/900 variants have updated <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#CFM56-7_series" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-7s</a>, a larger wing and an upgraded <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a>, and seat 108 to 215 passengers. The latest generation, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">737 MAX</a>, 737-7/8/9/10 MAX , powered by improved <a href="/wiki/CFM_LEAP" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM LEAP">CFM LEAP-1B</a> high bypass turbofans and accommodating 138 to 204 people, entered service in 2017.\n<a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a> versions are produced since the 737NG, as well as military models.\n</p><p>As of December 2019, 15,156 Boeing 737s have been ordered and 10,571 delivered.\nInitially, its main competitor was the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9">McDonnell Douglas DC-9</a>, followed by its <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-80" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-80">MD-80</a>/<a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-90" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-90">MD-90</a> derivatives. It was the highest-selling commercial aircraft until being surpassed by the competing <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a> in October 2019, but maintains the record in total deliveries. The 737 MAX, designed to compete with the <a href="/wiki/A320neo" class="mw-redirect" title="A320neo">A320neo</a>, was <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Boeing 737 MAX groundings">grounded worldwide</a> between March 2019 and November 2020 following two fatal crashes.<sup id="cite_ref-SeattleTimesUngrounding_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeattleTimesUngrounding-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" class="toctext">737-900</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#737_MAX_(fourth_generation)"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">737 MAX (fourth generation)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-20"><a href="#737_MAX_7"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">737 MAX 7</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#737_MAX_8"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">737 MAX 8</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#737_MAX_9"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.3</span> <span class="toctext">737 MAX 9</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#737_MAX_10"><span class="tocnumber">2.4.4</span> <span class="toctext">737 MAX 10</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Design"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Design</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Airframe"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Airframe</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Engines"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Engines</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Flight_systems"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Flight systems</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Aerodynamic"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Aerodynamic</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Interior"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Interior</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Other_variants"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Other variants</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#737_AEW&amp;C"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">737 AEW&amp;C</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#T-43/CT43A"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">T-43/CT43A</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#C-40_Clipper"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">C-40 Clipper</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#P-8_Poseidon"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">P-8 Poseidon</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Boeing_Business_Jet_(BBJ)"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Boeing Business Jet (BBJ)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Boeing_Converted_Freighter_program"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Boeing Converted Freighter program</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Competition"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Competition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Operators"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Operators</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Civilian"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Civilian</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Military"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Military</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Orders_and_deliveries"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Orders and deliveries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Accidents_and_incidents"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Accidents and incidents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Aircraft_on_display"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Aircraft on display</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Specifications"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Specifications</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1964_Boeing_737_concept.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/1964_Boeing_737_concept.png/220px-1964_Boeing_737_concept.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="86" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/1964_Boeing_737_concept.png/330px-1964_Boeing_737_concept.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6a/1964_Boeing_737_concept.png/440px-1964_Boeing_737_concept.png 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1964_Boeing_737_concept.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1964 concept with tail mounted engines</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg/220px-Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg/330px-Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg/440px-Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1014" data-file-height="676" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_patent_USD206035S.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>October 18, 1966, Jet aircraft patent, filed June 22, 1965, by John Steiner and Joe Sutter for Boeing</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-100_(N73700)_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-100_%28N73700%29_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2981" data-file-height="2355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-100_(N73700)_on_its_maiden_flight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737-100 on its maiden flight, April 9, 1967</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_design">Initial design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Initial design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Boeing had been studying short-haul jet aircraft designs, and saw a need for a new aircraft to supplement the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">727</a> on short and thin routes.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_transport_news_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_transport_news-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Preliminary design work began on May 11, 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001122_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001122-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> based on research that indicated a market for a fifty to sixty passenger airliner flying routes of 50 to 1,000&#160;mi (100 to 1,600&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_transport_news_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_transport_news-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200112_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200112-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The initial concept featured <a href="/wiki/Podded_engine" title="Podded engine">podded engines</a> on the aft fuselage, a <a href="/wiki/T-tail" title="T-tail">T-tail</a> as with the 727, and five-abreast seating. Engineer <a href="/wiki/Joe_Sutter" title="Joe Sutter">Joe Sutter</a> relocated the engines to the wings which lightened the wing structure and simplified the accommodation of six-abreast seating in the fuselage.<sup id="cite_ref-FG170406_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG170406-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The engine <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a> were mounted directly to the underside of the wings, without pylons, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a> to be shortened, thus lowering the fuselage to improve baggage and passenger access.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESutter200676\xe2\x80\x9378_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESutter200676\xe2\x80\x9378-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Relocating the engines from the aft fuselage also allowed the <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_stabilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Horizontal stabilizer">horizontal stabilizer</a> to be attached to the aft fuselage instead of as a T-tail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200117_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200117-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Many designs for the engine attachment strut were tested in the wind tunnel and the optimal shape for high speed was found to be one which was relatively thick, filling the narrow channels formed between the wing and the top of the nacelle, particularly on the outboard side.\n</p><p>At the time, Boeing was far behind its competitors; rival aircraft in service <a href="/wiki/Sud_Aviation_Caravelle" title="Sud Aviation Caravelle">SE 210 Caravelle</a> and in development, the <a href="/wiki/BAC_One-Eleven" title="BAC One-Eleven">BAC One-Eleven</a> (BAC-111), <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9">Douglas DC-9</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fokker_F28" class="mw-redirect" title="Fokker F28">Fokker F28</a> were already into flight certification.<sup id="cite_ref-german_order_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-german_order-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> To expedite development, Boeing used 60% of the structure and systems of the existing 727, the most notable being the fuselage, which differs in length only. This 148-inch (3.76 m) wide fuselage cross-section permitted six-abreast seating compared to the rivals\' five-abreast. The 727\'s fuselage was derived from the 707.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The proposed wing <a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">airfoil</a> sections were based on those of the 707 and 727, but somewhat thicker; altering these sections near the nacelles achieved a substantial drag reduction at high Mach numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The engine chosen was the <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_JT8D" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney JT8D">Pratt &amp; Whitney JT8D-1</a> low-bypass ratio turbofan engine, delivering 14,500&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">lbf</a> (64&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a>) thrust.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw19996_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw19996-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The concept design was presented in October 1964 at the <a href="/wiki/Air_Transport_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Transport Association">Air Transport Association</a> maintenance and engineering conference by chief project engineer Jack Steiner, where its elaborate <a href="/wiki/High-lift_device" title="High-lift device">high-lift devices</a> raised concerns about <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_maintenance" title="Aircraft maintenance">maintenance</a> costs and dispatch reliability.<sup id="cite_ref-FG170406_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG170406-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_design_developments">Major design developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Major design developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:B737Familyv1.0.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/B737Familyv1.0.png/400px-B737Familyv1.0.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/B737Familyv1.0.png/600px-B737Familyv1.0.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/B737Familyv1.0.png/800px-B737Familyv1.0.png 2x" data-file-width="2635" data-file-height="1473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:B737Familyv1.0.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of the first three generations</div></div></div>\n<p>The original 737 continued to be developed into thirteen passenger, cargo, corporate and military variants. These were later divided into what has become known as the four generations of the Boeing 737 family:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The first "Original" generation: the 737-100 and -200, also the military T-43 and C-43, launched February 1965.</li>\n<li>The second "Classic" generation: 737-300, -400 and 500 series, launched in 1979.</li>\n<li>The third generation "NG" series: 737-600, -700, -800 and 900 series, also the military C-40 and P-8, launched late 1993.</li>\n<li>The fourth generation 737 MAX series, launched August 2011.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The launch decision for the $150 million development was made by the board on February 1, 1965. <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> became the launch customer on February 19, 1965,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> with an order for 21 aircraft, worth $67&#160;million<sup id="cite_ref-german_order_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-german_order-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> after the airline had been assured by Boeing that the 737 project would not be canceled.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallace_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Consultation with Lufthansa over the previous winter had resulted in the seating capacity being increasd to 100.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200113-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On April 5, 1965, Boeing announced an order by <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> for 40 737s. United wanted a slightly larger capacity than the 737-100, so the fuselage was stretched 36&#160;in (91&#160;cm) ahead of, and 40&#160;in (102&#160;cm) behind the wing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200117_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200117-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The longer version was designated the 737-200, with the original short-body aircraft becoming the 737-100.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997182_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997182-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Detailed design work continued on both variants simultaneously.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-130,_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Lufthansa 737-100 in flight" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-130%2C_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-130,_Lufthansa_AN2056613.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737-100 introduced by Lufthansa on February 10, 1968.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first -100 was rolled out on January 17, 1967, and took its maiden flight on April 9, 1967, piloted by <a href="/wiki/Brien_Wygle" class="mw-redirect" title="Brien Wygle">Brien Wygle</a> and Lew Wallick.<sup id="cite_ref-737_30th_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_30th-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> After several test flights the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) issued Type Certificate A16WE certifying the 737-100 for commercial flight on December 15, 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Type_Cert_A16WE_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Type_Cert_A16WE-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It was the first aircraft to have, as part of its initial certification, approval for <a href="/wiki/Instrument_approach" title="Instrument approach">Category II approaches</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997183_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997183-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> which refers to a precision instrument approach and landing with a decision height between 98 to 197 feet (30 to 60&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Lufthansa received its first aircraft on December 28, 1967, and on February 10, 1968, became the first non-American airline to launch a new Boeing aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Lufthansa was the only significant customer to purchase the 737-100 and only 30 aircraft were produced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001120_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001120-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The -200 was rolled out on June 29, 1967, and had its maiden flight on August 8, 1967. It was then certified by the FAA on December 21, 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-Type_Cert_A16WE_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Type_Cert_A16WE-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001124_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001124-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The inaugural flight for United Airlines took place on April 28, 1968, from <a href="/wiki/O%27Hare_International_Airport" title="O&#39;Hare International Airport">Chicago</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gerald_R._Ford_International_Airport" title="Gerald R. Ford International Airport">Grand Rapids, Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The lengthened -200 was widely preferred over the -100 by airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The improved version, the 737-200 Advanced, was introduced into service by <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> on May 20, 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowers1989496_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowers1989496-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 737 original model with its variants, known later as the <i>Boeing 737 Original</i>, initially competed with SE 210 Caravelle and BAC-111 due to their earlier entry into service and later primarily with the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas" title="McDonnell Douglas">McDonnell Douglas</a> DC-9, then its <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-80" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-80">MD-80</a> derivatives as the three European short-haul single aisles slowly withdraw from the competition. Sales were low in the early 1970s<sup id="cite_ref-Flying_blind_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flying_blind-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and, after a peak of 114 deliveries in 1969, only 22 737s were shipped in 1972 with 19 in backlog. The <a href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="US Air Force">US Air Force</a> saved the program by ordering <a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">T-43s</a>, which were modified Boeing 737-200s. African airline orders kept the production running until the 1978 US <a href="/wiki/Airline_Deregulation_Act" title="Airline Deregulation Act">Airline Deregulation Act</a>, which improved demand for six-abreast narrow-body aircraft. Demand further increased after being re-engined with the <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FG170406_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG170406-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  The 737 went on to become the highest-selling commercial aircraft until surpassed by the competing <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a> in October 2019, but maintains the record in total deliveries.\n</p><p>The fuselage is manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>, by Boeing spin-off company <a href="/wiki/Spirit_AeroSystems" title="Spirit AeroSystems">Spirit AeroSystems</a>, before being moved by rail to Renton.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generations_and_variants">Generations and variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Generations and variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="737_Original_.28first_generation.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="737_Original_(first_generation)">737 Original (first generation)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 737 Original (first generation)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Boeing 737 Original</i> is the name given to the -100/200 and -200 Advanced series of the Boeing 737 family.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-100">737-100</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 737-100">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg/220px-Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg/330px-Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg/440px-Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2232" data-file-height="1243" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_NASA_GPN-2000-001905.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first Boeing 737-130 used as prototype and later operated by NASA</div></div></div>\n<p>The initial model was the 737-100, the smallest variant of the 737 aircraft family, which was launched in February 1965 and entered service with <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> in February 1968. In 1968, its unit cost was <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>3.7M (1968),<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek22jan1968_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek22jan1968-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> $27.2M today. A total of 30 737-100s were ordered: 22 by Lufthansa, 5 by <a href="/wiki/Malaysia%E2%80%93Singapore_Airlines" title="Malaysia\xe2\x80\x93Singapore Airlines">Malaysia\xe2\x80\x93Singapore Airlines</a> (MSA) and 2 by <a href="/wiki/Avianca" title="Avianca">Avianca</a> with the final commercial aircraft delivered to MSA on October 31, 1969. The first aircraft used by Boeing as prototype under registration N73700 was later ordered by and delivered to <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> on July 26, 1973, which then operated it under registration N515NA and retired after 30 years on September 27, 2003. This was the last operated 737-100 and is the only remaining worldwide, which is on the static display in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Flight</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw19998_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw19998-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original engine nacelles incorporated thrust reversers taken from the 727 outboard nacelles. They proved to be relatively ineffective and tended to lift the aircraft up off the runway when deployed. This reduced the downforce on the main wheels thereby reducing the effectiveness of the wheel brakes. In 1968, an improvement to the <a href="/wiki/Thrust_reversal" title="Thrust reversal">thrust reversal</a> system was introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsboeing737100200a00michpage21_21&#93;_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001[httpsarchiveorgdetailsboeing737100200a00michpage21_21]-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A 48-inch tailpipe extension was added and new, target-style, thrust reversers were incorporated. The thrust reverser doors were set 35 degrees away from the vertical to allow the exhaust to be deflected inboard and over the wings and outboard and under the wings. The improvement became standard on all aircraft after March 1969, and a retrofit was provided for active aircraft. Boeing fixed the drag issue by introducing new longer nacelle/wing fairings, and improved the airflow over the flaps and slats. The production line also introduced an improvement to the flap system, allowing increased use during takeoff and landing. All these changes gave the aircraft a boost to payload and range, and improved <a href="/wiki/Short-field_landing" title="Short-field landing">short-field</a> performance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200120-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-200">737-200</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 737-200">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-222,_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-222%2C_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="702" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-222,_United_Airlines_AN1045540.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737-200 was introduced by <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> on April 28, 1968.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-200 was a 737-100 with an extended fuselage, launched by an order from <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> in 1965 and entered service with the launch customer in April 1968. Its unit cost was US$4.0M (1968)<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek22jan1968_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek22jan1968-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> ($29.4M today). The -200\'s unit cost was US$5.2M (1972)<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> ($31.8M today). The 737-200 Advanced is an improved version of the -200, introduced into service by <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> on May 20, 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowers1989496_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowers1989496-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> After aircraft #135, the 737-200 Advanced has improved aerodynamics, automatic wheel brakes, more powerful engines, more fuel capacity, and hence a 15% increase in payload and range over the original -200s and respectively -100s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997183_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997183-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200141_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw200141-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The 737-200 Advanced became the production standard in June 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Boeing also provided the 737-200C (Combi), which allowed for conversion between passenger and cargo use and the 737-200QC (Quick Change), which facilitated a rapid conversion between roles. The 1,095th and last delivery of a -200 series aircraft was in August 1988 to <a href="/wiki/Xiamen_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="Xiamen Airlines">Xiamen Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-About_the_737_Family_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-About_the_737_Family-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nineteen 737-200s, designated <a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">T-43</a>, were used to train aircraft navigators for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a>. Some were modified into CT-43s, which are used to transport passengers, and one was modified as the NT-43A Radar Test Bed. The first was delivered on July 31, 1973, and the last on July 19, 1974. The <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Air_Force" title="Indonesian Air Force">Indonesian Air Force</a> ordered three modified 737-200s, designated <i>Boeing 737-2x9 Surveiller</i>. They were used as Maritime reconnaissance (MPA)/transport aircraft, fitted with SLAMMAR (Side-looking Multi-mission Airborne Radar). The aircraft were delivered between May 1982 and October 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowers1989498\xe2\x80\x93499_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowers1989498\xe2\x80\x93499-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After 40 years, in March 2008, the final 737-200 aircraft in the U.S. flying scheduled passenger service were phased out, with the last flights of <a href="/wiki/Aloha_Airlines" title="Aloha Airlines">Aloha Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-swretire_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-swretire-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The variant still sees regular service through North American charter operators such as <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Pacific_Airlines" title="Sierra Pacific Airlines">Sierra Pacific</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\nWith the improved short-field capabilities of the 737-200, Boeing offered the option of the <a href="/wiki/Gravelkit" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravelkit">gravel kit</a> modification features preventing <a href="/wiki/Foreign_object_damage" title="Foreign object damage">foreign object damage</a>, which enables this aircraft to operate on remote, unimproved or unpaved runways, such as gravel runways, that other similarly sized jetliner cannot.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Until retiring its -200 fleet in 2007, <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> used this option for some of its <a href="/wiki/Combi_aircraft" title="Combi aircraft">combi aircraft</a> rural operations to serve many unimproved runways in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Gravel-kitted 737-200 Combis are still used by <a href="/wiki/Canadian_North" title="Canadian North">Canadian North</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Inuit" title="Air Inuit">Air Inuit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nolinor_Aviation" title="Nolinor Aviation">Nolinor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrono_Aviation" title="Chrono Aviation">Chrono</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_North" title="Air North">Air North</a> in Northern Canada where gravel runways are common.\n</p><p>In July 2019, there were 46 Boeing 737-200s in service, mostly with "second and third tier" airlines, and those of developing nations.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="737_Classic_.28second_generation.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="737_Classic_(second_generation)">737 Classic (second generation)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 737 Classic (second generation)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">Boeing 737 Classic</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Boeing 737 Classic</i> is the name given to the 737-300/400/500 series after the introduction of the -600/700/800/900 series of the Boeing 737 family.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Produced from 1984 to 2000, a total of 1,988 Classic series were delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001129_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001129-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main development was to re-engine with the high pressure ratio CFM56-7.  By the early 1990s, while the MD-80 slowly withdraw from the competition leading to introduction of the MD-90, it had become clear that the new A320 family was a serious threat to Boeing\'s market share, as Airbus won previously loyal 737 customers such as Lufthansa and United Airlines. In November 1993, to keep the hand in the single aisle competition, Boeing\'s board of directors authorized the Next Generation program to mainly upgrade the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">737 Classic</a> series.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In late 1993, after engineering trade studies and discussions with major customers, Boeing proceeded to launch a second derivative of the Boeing 737, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">737 Next Generation</a> (NG) -600/700/800/900 series.<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It featured a redesigned wing with a wider wingspan and larger area, greater fuel capacity, longer range and higher <a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">MTOWs</a>. It was equipped with <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56#CFM56-7_series" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-7</a> high pressure ratio engines, a <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a>, and upgraded interior configurations. The four main models of the series can accommodate seating for 108 to 215 passengers. It was further developed into additional versions such as the corporate <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a> (BBJ) and military <a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">P-8 Poseidon</a> aircraft. Following the merger between Boeing with McDonnell Douglas in 1997, the primary competitor for the 737NG series remained only the A320 family.\n</p><p>Close to the next major upgrade of single aisle aircraft at Airbus and Boeing, the price of jet fuel reached a peak in 2008, when airlines devoted 40% of the retail price of an air ticket to pay for fuel, versus 15% in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, in that year carriers retired Boeing 737 Classic aircraft to reduce fuel consumption; replacements consisted of more efficient 737 Next Generation or A320 family aircraft. On June 4, 2008, <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> announced it would retire all 94 of its Classic 737 aircraft (64 737-300 and 30 737-500 aircraft), replacing them with A320 family jets taken from its <a href="/wiki/Ted_(airline)" title="Ted (airline)">Ted</a> subsidiary, which has been shut down.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This intensified the competition between the two giant aircraft manufacturers, which has since become a <a href="/wiki/Competition_between_Airbus_and_Boeing" title="Competition between Airbus and Boeing">duopoly competition</a>.\n</p><p>An optional upgrade with winglets became available for the Classic and NG series.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The 737-300 can be retrofitted with <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners" title="Aviation Partners">Aviation Partners</a> Boeing winglets. The 737-300 retrofitted with winglets is designated the -300SP (Special Performance).</li>\n<li>WestJet was to launch the 737-600 with winglets, but dropped them in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-300">737-300</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 737-300">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USAir_Boeing_737-3B7;_N524AU@LAS;01.08.1995_(5027148379).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg/220px-USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg/330px-USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg/440px-USAir_Boeing_737-3B7%3B_N524AU%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%285027148379%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2620" data-file-height="1766" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USAir_Boeing_737-3B7;_N524AU@LAS;01.08.1995_(5027148379).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737-300 with wider <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56</a> turbofans, introduced by <a href="/wiki/USAir" class="mw-redirect" title="USAir">USAir</a> on November 28, 1984.</div></div></div>\n<p>Development began in 1979 for the 737\'s first major revision, which was originally introduced as the \'new generation\' of the 737.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw19997_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw19997-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Boeing wanted to increase capacity and range, incorporating improvements to upgrade the aircraft to modern specifications, while also retaining commonality with previous 737 variants. In 1980, preliminary aircraft specifications of the variant, dubbed 737-300, were released at the <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001126_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001126-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> This first major upgrade series was later renamed to the 737 Classic series. It competed primarily with MD-80, later its derivative <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-90" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-90">MD-90</a> and the newcomer <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a>.\nBoeing engineer Mark Gregoire led a design team, which cooperated with <a href="/wiki/CFM_International" title="CFM International">CFM International</a> to select, modify and deploy a new engine and nacelle that would make the 737-300 into a viable aircraft. They chose the <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-3B-1</a> <a href="/wiki/Bypass_ratio" title="Bypass ratio">high-bypass</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engine to power the aircraft, which yielded significant gains in fuel economy and a reduction in noise, but also posed an engineering challenge, given the low ground clearance of the 737 and the larger diameter of the engine over the original Pratt &amp; Whitney engines. Gregoire\'s team and CFM solved the problem by reducing the size of the fan (which made the engine slightly less efficient than it had been forecast to be), placing the engine ahead of the wing, and by moving engine accessories to the sides of the engine pod, giving the engine a distinctive non-circular "hamster <a href="/wiki/Cheek_pouch" title="Cheek pouch">pouch</a>" air intake.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Earlier customers for the CFM56 included the U.S. Air Force with its program to re-engine KC-135 tankers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarvin1998137_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarvin1998137-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\nThe passenger capacity of the aircraft was increased to 149 by extending the fuselage around the wing by 9&#160;feet 5&#160;inches (2.87&#160;m). The wing incorporated several changes for improved aerodynamics. The wingtip was extended 9&#160;in (23&#160;cm), and the wingspan by 1&#160;ft 9&#160;in (53&#160;cm). The leading-edge slats and trailing-edge flaps were adjusted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The tailfin was redesigned, the flight deck was improved with the optional EFIS (Electronic Flight Instrumentation System), and the passenger cabin incorporated improvements similar to those developed on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199910_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199910-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The prototype -300, the 1,001st 737 built, first flew on February 24, 1984, with pilot Jim McRoberts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199910_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199910-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> It and two production aircraft flew a nine-month-long certification program.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199912\xe2\x80\x9313_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199912\xe2\x80\x9313-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The 737-300 retrofitted with <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners" title="Aviation Partners">Aviation Partners</a>\' winglets was designated the -300SP (<i>Special Performance</i>). The 737-300 was replaced by the 737-700 of the Next Generation series.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-400">737-400</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 737-400">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alaskan_Freightdog_(40872772912).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg/220px-Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg/330px-Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg/440px-Alaskan_Freightdog_%2840872772912%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1152" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alaskan_Freightdog_(40872772912).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alaska Airlines was the first airline to have a 737-400 converted to a freighter.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-400 was launched in 1985 to fill the gap between the 737-300 and the 757-200. In June 1986, Boeing announced the development of the 737-400,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997185_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReddingYenne1997185-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> which stretched the fuselage a further 10&#160;ft (3.0&#160;m), increasing the capacity to 188 passenger and required a tail bumper to prevent <a href="/wiki/Tailstrike" title="Tailstrike">tailstrikes</a> during take-off, and a strengthened <a href="/wiki/Wing_spar" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing spar">wing spar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rgl.faa.gov_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rgl.faa.gov-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The -400s first flight was on February 19, 1988, and, after a seven-month/500-hour flight-testing run, entered service with <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_Airlines_(1948-1989)" class="mw-redirect" title="Piedmont Airlines (1948-1989)">Piedmont Airlines</a> that October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199914_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199914-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The last two -400s, i.e. the last 737 Classics series, were delivered to <a href="/wiki/CSA_Czech_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="CSA Czech Airlines">CSA Czech Airlines</a> on February 28, 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The 737-400 was replaced by the 737-800 of the Next Generation series. The 737-400SF was a 737-400 converted to freighter, though it was not a model delivered by Boeing and hence the nickname <i>Special Freighter (SF)</i>. <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> was the first to convert one of their 400s from regular service to an aircraft with the ability to handle 10 pallets.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The airline had also converted five more into fixed combi aircraft for half passenger and freight. These 737-400 Combi aircraft were retired in 2017 and replaced by the 737-700F of the Next Generation series.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-500">737-500</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 737-500">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500;_N519SW@LAS;01.08.1995_(4905698533).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg/220px-Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg/330px-Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg/440px-Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500%3B_N519SW%40LAS%3B01.08.1995_%284905698533%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5492" data-file-height="3701" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southwest_Airlines_Boeing_737-500;_N519SW@LAS;01.08.1995_(4905698533).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shorter by 7&#160;ft 10&#160;in (2.4&#160;m), <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> received the first 737-500 on February 28, 1990</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-500 was offered as a modern and direct replacement of the 737-200. It was launched in 1987 by <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a>, with an order for 20 aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199940_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199940-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and it flew for the first time on June 30, 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199914_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199914-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> A single prototype flew 375 hours for the certification process,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199914_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199914-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and on February 28, 1990, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> received the first delivery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001129_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001129-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The -500 incorporated the improvements of the 737 Classic series, allowing longer routes with fewer passengers to be more economical than with the 737-300. The fuselage length of the 737-500 is 1&#160;ft 7&#160;in (48&#160;cm) longer than the 737-200, accommodating up to 140<sup id="cite_ref-rgl.faa.gov_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rgl.faa.gov-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> passengers. Both glass and older-style mechanical cockpits arrangements were available.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199914_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199914-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Using the CFM56-3 engine also gave a 25% increase in <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a> over the older 737-200s P&amp;W engines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw199914_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw199914-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\nThe 737-500 has faced accelerated retirement due to its smaller size, after 21 years in service compared to 24 years for the -300.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> While a few 737-300s were slated for freighter conversion, no demand at all existed for a -500 freighter conversion. The 737-500 was replaced by the 737-600 of the Next Generation series, though the -600 was not as successful in total orders as the -500.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="737_NG_.28third_generation.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="737_NG_(third_generation)">737 NG (third generation)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 737 NG (third generation)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737 Next Generation</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Boeing 737 Next Generation</i>, abbreviated as <i>737 Next Gen</i> or <i>737NG</i>, is the name given to the main models 737-600/700/800/900 series and the extended range -700ER/900ER variants of the Boeing 737 family. It has been produced since 1996 and introduced in 1997, with a total order of 7,097 aircraft, of which 7,031 have been delivered as of May&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flying_blind_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flying_blind-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-600">737-600</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 737-600">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/220px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/330px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg/440px-Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2517" data-file-height="1679" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sas_b737-600_ln-rcw_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SAS received the first 737-600 in September 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-600, the smallest model of the Next-Generation, was launched by <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">Scandinavian Airlines</a> (SAS) in March 1995 with the first aircraft delivered in September 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> A total of 69 aircraft without <a href="/wiki/Winglets" class="mw-redirect" title="Winglets">winglets</a> have been produced with the last one delivered to <a href="/wiki/WestJet" title="WestJet">WestJet</a> in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The 737-600 replaced the 737-500 and is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A318" title="Airbus A318">Airbus A318</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-700">737-700</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 737-700">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/220px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/330px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg/440px-N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3420" data-file-height="2154" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:N785SW_BWI_MD1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southwest Airlines took delivery of the first 737-700 in December 1997</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-700, the first variant of the Next-Generation, was launched in November 1993 with an order of 63 aircraft. The -700 seats 126 passengers in a two-class or 149 passengers in a one-class layout. The launch customer <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> took the first delivery in December 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing17dec1997_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing17dec1997-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The 737-700 replaced the 737-300 and competes with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319" title="Airbus A319">Airbus A319</a>.\n</p><p>The <i>737-700C</i> is a convertible version where the seats can be removed to carry cargo instead. There is a large door on the left side of the aircraft. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> was the launch customer for the 737-700C under the military designation <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">C-40 Clipper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>737-700ER</i> (Extended <a href="/wiki/Range_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Range (aircraft)">Range</a>) was launched on January 31, 2006, and featured the fuselage of the 737-700 and the wings and landing gear of the 737-800. A 737-700ER can typically accommodate 126 passengers in two classes with a range similar to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319LR" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus A319LR">Airbus A319LR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-800">737-800</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 737-800">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_(6341092368).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/220px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/330px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg/440px-D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_%286341092368%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3191" data-file-height="2127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D-ATUF_B737-8K5W_TUIfly-Hapag_retro_PMI_26SEP10_(6341092368).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hapagfly" class="mw-redirect" title="Hapagfly">Hapag-Lloyd</a> received the first 737-800 in April 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>The Boeing 737-800 was a stretched version of the 737-700 launched on September 5, 1994. The -800 seats 162 passengers in a two-class or 189 passengers in a high-density, one-class layout. Launch customer <a href="/wiki/Hapagfly" class="mw-redirect" title="Hapagfly">Hapag-Lloyd Flug</a> (now <a href="/wiki/TUIfly" class="mw-redirect" title="TUIfly">TUIfly</a>) received the first one in April 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The -800 replaced directly the -400 and aging 727-200 of US airlines. It filled also the gap left by Boeing\'s decision to discontinue the MD-80 and <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-90" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-90">MD-90</a> aircraft, following Boeing\'s merger with McDonnell Douglas. The 737-800 is the most widely used narrowbody aircraft and competes primarily with the Airbus A320.<sup id="cite_ref-B738_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B738-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737-900">737-900</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 737-900">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EM_N323AS_(2896342517).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/220px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/330px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg/440px-EM_N323AS_%282896342517%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1727" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EM_N323AS_(2896342517).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first 737-900 was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> on May 15, 2001</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737-900 was launched in 1997. It retains the <a href="/wiki/Maximum_Takeoff_Weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Maximum Takeoff Weight">MTOW</a>, fuel capacity, trading range for payload and also the exit configuration of the -800, limiting its seat capacity to approximately 177 in a two class and 189 in a high-density, one class layout. The launch customer <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> received the delivery on May 15, 2001.\n</p><p>The <i>737-900ER</i> (Extended Range) is the newest and largest variant of the 737NG generation. An additional pair of exit doors and a flat <a href="/wiki/Rear_pressure_bulkhead" class="mw-redirect" title="Rear pressure bulkhead">rear pressure bulkhead</a> increased its seating capacity to 180 passengers in a two-class and up to 220 passengers in a one-class configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The -900ER was introduced to meet the range and passenger capacity of the discontinued <a href="/wiki/757-200" class="mw-redirect" title="757-200">757-200</a> and to directly compete with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A321" title="Airbus A321">Airbus A321</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="737_MAX_.28fourth_generation.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_(fourth_generation)">737 MAX (fourth generation)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 737 MAX (fourth generation)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Boeing 737 MAX</i> is the name given to the main MAX 737-7/8/9/10 series and high-density MAX 200 variant of the Boeing 737 family. It is offered in four main variants, typically offering 138 to 230 seats and a range of 3,215 to 3,825&#160;nmi (5,954 to 7,084&#160;km). The 737 MAX&#160;7, MAX&#160;8 (including the denser, 200-seat MAX&#160;200), and MAX&#160;9 replace the 737-700, -800, and -900 respectively. The further stretched 737 MAX&#160;10 has also been added to the series.\nThe main development was to re-engine with CFM LEAP-1B very high bypass ratio.  On July 20, 2011, Boeing announced plans for a third major upgrade and respectively fourth generation of 737 series to be powered by the <a href="/wiki/CFM_LEAP" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM LEAP">CFM LEAP-1B</a> engine, with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a> intending to order 100 of these aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 30, 2011, Boeing confirmed the launch of the 737 new engine variant, to be called the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX" title="Boeing 737 MAX">Boeing 737 MAX</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\nIt was based on earlier 737 designs with more efficient LEAP-1B power plants, aerodynamic improvements (most notably split-tip <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">winglets</a>), and airframe modifications. It competes with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo family">Airbus A320neo family</a> that was launched in December 2010 and reached 1,029 orders by June 2011, breaking Boeing\'s monopoly with <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, which had an order for 130 A320neos that July.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The 737 MAX had its first flight on January 29, 2016, and gained FAA certification on March 8, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-ST160129_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST160129-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FAA-TCDS_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAA-TCDS-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The first delivery was a MAX&#160;8 on May 6, 2017, to <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air" title="Lion Air">Lion Air</a>\'s subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Flight16may2017_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight16may2017-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> which put it into service on May 22, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight22may2017_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22may2017-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> As of January&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the series has received 5,011 firm orders.<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2019, <a href="/wiki/National_aviation_authority" title="National aviation authority">aviation authorities</a> around the world <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Boeing 737 MAX groundings">grounded the 737 MAX</a> following two <a href="/wiki/Hull_loss" title="Hull loss">hull loss</a> crashes which caused 346 deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-maxgroundings_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-maxgroundings-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> On December 16, 2019, Boeing announced that it would suspend production of the 737 MAX from January 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing16dec2019_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing16dec2019-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> which was resumed in May 2020. In the midyear 2020, the FAA and Boeing conducted a series of recertification test flights.<sup id="cite_ref-FAAflighttestscompleted_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAAflighttestscompleted-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> On November 18, 2020, the FAA cleared the MAX to return to service. Before the aircraft can fly again, repairs must be implemented and airlines\' training programs must be approved. Passenger flights in the U.S. are expected to resume before the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-SeattleTimesUngrounding_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeattleTimesUngrounding-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> \nWorldwide, the first airline to resume passenger service was Brazilian low-cost <a href="/wiki/Gol_Transportes_A%C3%A9reos" class="mw-redirect" title="Gol Transportes A\xc3\xa9reos">Gol</a>, on December 9, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_7">737 MAX 7</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 7">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_(43406207022).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/220px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/330px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/440px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2353" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_(43406207022).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 MAX 7 at the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The 737 MAX 7, a shortened variant of the MAX&#160;8, was originally based on the 737-700, flying 1,000&#160;nmi (1,900&#160;km) farther and accommodating two more seat rows at 18% lower fuel costs per seat.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FG_Max_7_redesign_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG_Max_7_redesign-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The redesign uses the 737-8 wing and landing gear; a pair of over-wing exits rather than the single-door configuration; a 46-inch longer aft fuselage and a 30-inch longer forward fuselage; structural re-gauging and strengthening; and systems and interior modifications to accommodate the longer length.<sup id="cite_ref-AIN160711_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIN160711-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Entry into service with launch operator Southwest Airlines was expected in January 2019\' but the airline deferred these orders until 2023\xe2\x80\x932024.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight4oct2017_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight4oct2017-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Flight2Jan2018_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight2Jan2018-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The 737 MAX 7 replaced the 737-700 and was predicted to carry 12 more passengers and fly 400&#160;nmi (740&#160;km) farther than <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A319neo" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus A319neo">Airbus A319neo</a> with 7% lower operating costs per seat.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek16mar2018_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek16mar2018-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_8">737 MAX 8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:9M-LRD_(37495597164).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/220px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="92" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/330px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/440px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2801" data-file-height="1168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:9M-LRD_(37495597164).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Boeing 737 MAX 8 entered service with Malindo Air (wearing <a href="/wiki/Batik_Air_Malaysia" class="mw-redirect" title="Batik Air Malaysia">Batik Air Malaysia</a> livery)</div></div></div>\n<p>The MAX 8, the first variant of the 737 MAX, has a longer fuselage than the MAX 7. On July 23, 2013, Boeing completed the firm configuration for the 737 MAX 8.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Its first commercial flight was operated by <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a> on May 22, 2017.
