The MAX 8 replaced the 737-800 and competed with the A320neo.\n</p><p>The 737 MAX 200, a high-density version of the 737 MAX 8, was launched in September 2014 and named for seating for up to 200 passengers in a single-class layout with <a href="/wiki/Airline_seat#&quot;Slimline&quot;_economy_seating" title="Airline seat">slimline seats</a> requiring an extra pair of <a href="/wiki/Emergency_exit#Aircraft" title="Emergency exit">exit doors</a>. The MAX 200 would be 20% more cost-efficient per seat, including 5% lower operating costs than the MAX 8 and would be the most efficient narrow-body on the market when entering service.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In mid-November 2018, the first MAX 200 of the 135 ordered by Ryanair rolled out, in a 197-seat configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> It was first flown from Renton on January 13, 2019, and was due to enter service in April 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_9">737 MAX 9</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 9">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing,_N7379E,_Boeing_737-9_MAX_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2104" data-file-height="1446" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing,_N7379E,_Boeing_737-9_MAX_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 MAX 9 first flight on April 13, 2017</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737 MAX 9, the stretched variant of the MAX 8, was launched with an order of 201 aircraft in February 2012. It made its roll-out on March 7, 2017, and first flight on April 13, 2017;<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> It was certified by February 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The launch customer, Lion Air Group, took the first MAX 9 on March 21, 2018, before entering service with <a href="/wiki/Thai_Lion_Air" title="Thai Lion Air">Thai Lion Air</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing21mar2018_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing21mar2018-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The 737 MAX 9 replaced the 737-900 and competed with <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A321neo" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus A321neo">Airbus A321neo</a>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_10">737 MAX 10</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 10">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg/220px-Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/22/Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg/330px-Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/22/Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="421" data-file-height="236" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_MAX_10X_rendering.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 MAX 10 rendering</div></div></div>\n<p>The MAX 10 was proposed as a stretched MAX 9 in mid-2016, enabling seating for 230 in a single class or 189 in two-class layout, compared to 193 in two-class seating for the A321neo. The modest 66&#160;in (1.7&#160;m) stretch of fuselage enables the MAX 10 to retain the existing wing and CFM Leap 1B engine from the MAX 9 with a trailing-link main landing gear as the only major change.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The MAX 10 was launched on June 19, 2017, with 240 orders and commitments from more than ten customers.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The variant configuration with a predicted 5% lower trip cost and seat cost compared to the A321neo was firmed up by February 2018, and by mid-2018, the <a href="/wiki/Critical_design_review" class="mw-redirect" title="Critical design review">critical design review</a> was completed.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The Max 10 has similar capacity as A321XLR, but shorter range and much poorer field performance in smaller airports than A321XLR.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> It was unveiled in Boeing\'s Renton factory on November 22, 2019, and scheduled for the first flight in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Boeing also considered a parallel development along with the 757 replacement, similar to the development of the 757 and 767 in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-awnNeo_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-awnNeo-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 2010s, Boeing worked on a medium-range <a href="/wiki/Boeing_New_Midsize_Airplane" title="Boeing New Midsize Airplane">Boeing New Midsize Airplane</a> (NMA) with two variants seating 225 or 275 passengers and targeting the same market segment as the 737 MAX 10 and the Airbus A321 neo.<sup id="cite_ref-aw20180207_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aw20180207-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A Future Small Airplane (FSA) was also touted during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-aircurrent-20191028_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aircurrent-20191028-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\nThe NMA project was set aside in January 2020, as Boeing focused on returning the 737 MAX to service and announced that it would be taking a new approach to future projects.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight23jan2020_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight23jan2020-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The 737 continued to evolve into many variants but still remains recognisable as the 737. These are divided into four generations but all are based on the same basic design.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airframe">Airframe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Airframe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The fuselage cross section and nose is derived from that of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">Boeing 707</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">Boeing 727</a>. Early 737 cockpits also inherited the "eyebrow windows" positioned above the main glareshield, which were a feature of the original 707 and 727<sup id="cite_ref-eyebrow_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eyebrow-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> to allow for better crew visibility.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to popular belief, these windows were not intended for <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> (only the military T-43A had a sextant port for star navigation, which the civilian models lacked.)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsboeing737100200a00michpage37_37&#93;_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharpeShaw2001[httpsarchiveorgdetailsboeing737100200a00michpage37_37]-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> With modern avionics, the windows became redundant, and many pilots actually placed newspapers or other objects in them to block out sun glare. They were eliminated from the 737 cockpit design in 2004, although they are still installed on customer request.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The eyebrow windows were sometimes removed and plugged, usually during maintenance overhauls, and can be distinguished by the metal plug which differs from the smooth metal in later aircraft that were not originally fitted with the windows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 737\'s main landing gear, under the wings at mid-cabin, rotates into wheel wells in the aircraft\'s belly. The legs are covered by partial doors, and "brush-like" seals aerodynamically smooth (or "fair") the wheels in the wells. The sides of the tires are exposed to the air in flight. "Hub caps" complete the aerodynamic profile of the wheels. It is forbidden to operate without the caps, because they are linked to the ground speed sensor that interfaces with the anti-skid brake system. The dark circles of the tires are clearly visible when a 737 takes off, or is at low altitude.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From July 2008 the steel landing gear <a href="/wiki/Brake" title="Brake">brakes</a> on new NGs were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Messier-Bugatti" class="mw-redirect" title="Messier-Bugatti">Messier-Bugatti</a> carbon brakes, achieving weight savings up to 550\xe2\x80\x93700&#160;lb (250\xe2\x80\x93320&#160;kg) depending on whether standard or high-capacity brakes.<sup id="cite_ref-cfb_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cfb-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> On a 737-800 this gives a 0.5% improvement in fuel efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>737s are not equipped with <a href="/wiki/Fuel_dump_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuel dump systems">fuel dump systems</a>. The original design was too small to require this, and adding a fuel dump system to the later, larger variants would have incurred a large weight penalty. Boeing instead demonstrated an "equivalent level of safety". Depending upon the nature of the emergency, 737s either circle to burn off fuel or land overweight. If the latter is the case, the aircraft is inspected by maintenance personnel for damage and then returned to service if none is found.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 192px"><div style="width: 192px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 190px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-200_planform_view.jpg" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The original 737 with <a href="/wiki/JT8D" class="mw-redirect" title="JT8D">JT8D</a> engines that span the entire <a /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/737_Classic" class="mw-redirect" title="737 Classic">737 Classic</a> with stubbier and wider <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56s</a> ahead of the wings\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 184px"><div style="width: 184px">\n\t\t\t<div 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg/546px-Boeing_737-79P%2C_China_Eastern_Airlines_JP6128143.jpg 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="738" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/737NG" class="mw-redirect" title="737NG">737NG</a> has a 25% larger and 16&#160;ft (5 m) wider wing\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 161.33333333333px"><div style="width: 161.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 159.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:PK-LQJ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/PK-LQJ.jpg/239px-PK-LQJ.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="160" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/PK-LQJ.jpg/359px-PK-LQJ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/PK-LQJ.jpg/479px-PK-LQJ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="1367" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/737_MAX" class="mw-redirect" title="737 MAX">737 MAX</a> has larger <a href="/wiki/CFM_LEAP" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM LEAP">CFM LEAP</a> engines with chevrons\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Engines on the 737 Classic series (-300, -400, -500) and Next-Generation series (-600, -700, -800, -900) do not have circular inlets like most aircraft but rather a planform on the lower side, which has been dictated largely by the need to accommodate ever larger engine diameters. The 737 Classic series featured <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56</a> high bypass <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engines, which yielded significant gains in <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_aircraft" title="Fuel economy in aircraft">fuel economy</a> and a reduction in noise over the <a href="/wiki/JT8D" class="mw-redirect" title="JT8D">JT8D</a> low bypass engines used on the 737 Original series (-100 and -200), but also posed an engineering challenge given the low ground clearance of the Boeing 737 family. Boeing and engine supplier <a href="/wiki/CFM_International" title="CFM International">CFM International</a> (CFMI) solved the problem by placing the engine ahead of (rather than below) the wing, and by moving engine accessories to the sides (rather than the bottom) of the engine pod, giving the 737 Classic and later generations a distinctive non-circular air intake.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEndres2001128-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The wing also incorporated changes for improved aerodynamics. The engines\' accessory <a href="/wiki/Gearbox" class="mw-redirect" title="Gearbox">gearbox</a> was moved from the 6 o\'clock position under the engine to the 4 o\'clock position (from a front/forward looking aft perspective). This side-mounted gearbox gives the engine a somewhat rounded triangular shape. Because the engine is close to the ground, 737-300s and later models are more prone to engine <a href="/wiki/Foreign_object_damage" title="Foreign object damage">foreign object damage</a> (FOD). The improved, higher pressure ratio <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56" title="CFM International CFM56">CFM56-7</a> turbofan engine on the 737 Next Generation is 7% more fuel-efficient than the previous CFM56-3 on the 737 Classic with the same bypass ratio. The newest 737 variants, the 737 MAX series, feature <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP-X" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM International LEAP-X">LEAP-1B</a> engines from CFMI with a 68 inches (1.73&#160;m) fan diameter. These engines were expected to be 10-12% more efficient than the CFM56-7B engines on the 737 Next Generation series.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti CFM56 engine with ovoid "hamster pouch" inlet</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:186px;max-width:186px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg/368px-Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="406" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">737 MAX 9 CFM LEAP-1B engine with 787-derived engine chevrons</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_systems">Flight systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Flight systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 737 is unusual in that it still uses a <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_flight_control_system#Hydro-mechanical" title="Aircraft flight control system">hydro-mechanical</a> flight control system, similar to the Boeing 707 and typical of the period, that transmits pilot commands to control surfaces by steel cables run through the fuselage and wings rather than by an electrical <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> system as used in all of the Airbus fleet and all later Boeing models.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> This has been raised as a safety issue because of the impracticality of duplicating such a mechanical cable-based system in the way that an electrical or electronic system can be. This leaves the flight controls as a single point of failure, for example by metal fragments from an uncontained engine failure penetrating the wings or fuselage.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_flight_control_system" title="Aircraft flight control system">primary flight controls</a> have mechanical backups.  In the event of total hydraulic system failure or double engine failure, they will automatically and seamlessly revert to control via <a href="/wiki/Servo_tab" title="Servo tab">servo tab</a>. In this mode, the servo tabs aerodynamically control the elevators and ailerons; these servo tabs are in turn controlled by cables running to the control yoke. The pilot\'s muscle forces alone control the tabs.\n</p><p>The 737 Next Generation series introduced a six-screen LCD <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> with modern avionics but designed to retain crew commonality with previous 737 generations.<sup id="cite_ref-sixscreen_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sixscreen-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;max-width:552px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Boeing_737-2V6_Advanced_Cockpit_Dubai_Air_Show_Ryabtsev.jpg/180px-Boeing_737-2V6_Advanced_Cockpit_Dubai_Air_Show_Ryabtsev.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Boeing_737-2V6_Advanced_Cockpit_Dubai_Air_Show_Ryabtsev.jpg/270px-Boeing_737-2V6_Advanced_Cockpit_Dubai_Air_Show_Ryabtsev.jpg 1.5x, data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="799" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Original 737-200 cockpit</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/S7_Airlines_Boeing_737-8ZS_flight_deck_Beltyukov.jpg/360px-S7_Airlines_Boeing_737-8ZS_flight_deck_Beltyukov.jpg 2x" data-file-width="877" data-file-height="584" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Next Generation 737-800 cockpit</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerodynamic">Aerodynamic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Aerodynamic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Original -100 and -200 series were built without <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_devices" class="mw-redirect" title="Wingtip devices">wingtip devices</a> but these were later introduced to improve fuel efficiency. The 737 has evolved four winglet types: the 737-200 Mini-winglet, 737 Classic/NG Blended Winglet, 737 Split Scimitar Winglet, and 737 MAX Advanced Technology Winglet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The 737-200 Mini-winglets are part of the Quiet Wing Corp modification kit that received certification in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145_115-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2014144\xe2\x80\x93145-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Blended <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">winglets</a> were standard on the 737 NG and are available for retrofit on 737 Classic models. These winglets stand approximately 8 feet (2.4&#160;m) tall and are installed at the wing tips. They improve fuel efficiency by up to 5% through lift-induced drag reduction achieved by moderating <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_vortices" title="Wingtip vortices">wingtip vortices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blended_winglets_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blended_winglets-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Split Scimitar winglets became available in 2014 for the 737-800, 737-900ER, BBJ2 and BBJ3, and in 2015 for the 737-700, 737-900 and BBJ1.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Split Scimitar winglets were developed by <a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners" title="Aviation Partners">Aviation Partners</a>, the same Seattle-based corporation that developed the blended winglets; the Split Scimitar winglets produce up to a 5.5% fuel savings per aircraft compared to 3.3% savings for the blended winglets. Southwest Airlines flew their first flight of a 737-800 with Split Scimitar winglets on April 14, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The next generation 737, 737 MAX, will feature an Advanced Technology (AT) Winglet that is produced by Boeing. The Boeing AT Winglet resembles a cross between the Blended Winglet and the Split Scimitar Winglet.<sup id="cite_ref-modwinglet_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modwinglet-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An optional <a href="#Enhanced_Short_Runway_Package">Enhanced Short Runway Package</a> was developed for use on short runways.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:533px;max-width:533px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:168px;max-width:168px"><div class="thumbimage"><a //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Boeing_737_MAX_%2823326959580%29_winglet.jpg/338px-Boeing_737_MAX_%2823326959580%29_winglet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2852" data-file-height="1973" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Advanced Technology Winglet standard on MAX</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Interior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first generation Original series 737 cabin was replaced for the second generation Classic series with a design based on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757</a> cabin. The Classic cabin was then redesigned once more for the third, Next Generation, 737 with a design based on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a> cabin. Boeing later offered the redesigned Sky Interior on the NG.\nThe principle features of the Sky Interior include: sculpted sidewalls, redesigned window housings, increased headroom and LED <a href="/wiki/Mood_lighting" title="Mood lighting">mood lighting</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-margins_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-margins-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-skyinterior_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skyinterior-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> larger pivot-bins based on the 777 and 787 designs and generally more luggage space,<sup id="cite_ref-skyinterior_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skyinterior-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> and claims to have improved cabin noise levels by 2\xe2\x80\x934 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">dB</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-margins_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-margins-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\nThe first 737 equipped Boeing Sky Interior was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Flydubai" title="Flydubai">Flydubai</a> in late 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-margins_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-margins-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Continental Airlines,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malaysia_Airlines" title="Malaysia Airlines">Malaysia Airlines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/TUIFly" class="mw-redirect" title="TUIFly">TUIFly</a> have also received Sky Interior-equipped 737s.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:539px;max-width:539px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg/167px-Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg" decoding="async" width="167" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg/251px-Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg/334px-Lufthansa_737_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="737" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">737 Classic interior in 3\xe2\x80\x933 economy class layout</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg/180px-Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg/270px-Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg/360px-Delta_Air_Lines_Boeing_737-800_cabin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Boeing 737NG standard interior with curved panels</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Malaysia_Airlines_738_Economy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Malaysia_Airlines_738_Economy.jpg/180px-Malaysia_Airlines_738_Economy.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Malaysia_Airlines_738_Economy.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="133" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Boeing 737NG Sky Interior with pivot bins and LED lighting</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_variants">Other variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="737_AEW.26C"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="737_AEW&amp;C">737 AEW&amp;C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 737 AEW&amp;C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_(7445565660).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/170px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/255px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg/340px-%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_%287445565660%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2558" data-file-height="1707" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%EA%B3%B5%EC%A4%91%EC%A1%B0%EA%B8%B0%EA%B2%BD%EB%B3%B4%ED%86%B5%EC%A0%9C%EA%B8%B0_(7445565660).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Airborne_Early_Warning_and_Control" class="mw-redirect" title="Airborne Early Warning and Control">Airborne Early Warning and Control</a> version of the 737-700</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_AEW%26C" title="Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C">Boeing 737 AEW&amp;C</a> is a 737-700IGW roughly similar to the 737-700ER. This is an <a href="/wiki/Airborne_Early_Warning_and_Control" class="mw-redirect" title="Airborne Early Warning and Control">Airborne Early Warning and Control</a> (AEW&amp;C) version of the 737NG. <a href="/wiki/Royal_Australian_Air_Force" title="Royal Australian Air Force">Australia</a> is the first customer (as Project Wedgetail), followed by <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and South Korea.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="T-43.2FCT43A"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="T-43/CT43A">T-43/CT43A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: T-43/CT43A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:T-43_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/T-43_2.jpg/170px-T-43_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/T-43_2.jpg/255px-T-43_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/T-43_2.jpg/340px-T-43_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="986" data-file-height="655" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:T-43_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">T-43</a> was a 737-200 used by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> to train navigators</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">Boeing T-43</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_T-43" title="Boeing T-43">T-43</a> was a 737-200 modified for use by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> for training navigators, now known as USAF <a href="/wiki/Combat_systems_officer" title="Combat systems officer">combat systems officers</a>. Informally referred to as the <i>Gator</i> (an abbreviation of "navigator") and "Flying Classroom", nineteen of these aircraft were delivered to the <a href="/wiki/Air_Training_Command" title="Air Training Command">Air Training Command</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mather_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Mather AFB">Mather AFB</a>, California during 1973 and 1974.  Two additional aircraft were delivered to the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Air_National_Guard" title="Colorado Air National Guard">Colorado Air National Guard</a> at <a href="/wiki/Buckley_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Buckley AFB">Buckley ANGB</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Buckley_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Buckley AFB">Buckley AFB</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Peterson_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Peterson AFB">Peterson AFB</a>, Colorado, in direct support of cadet air navigation training at the nearby <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Air Force Academy">U.S. Air Force Academy</a>.\n</p><p>Two T-43s were later converted to CT-43As, similar to the CT-40A Clipper below, in the early 1990s and transferred to <a href="/wiki/Air_Mobility_Command" title="Air Mobility Command">Air Mobility Command</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Forces_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Air Forces in Europe">United States Air Forces in Europe</a>, respectively, as executive transports. A third aircraft was also transferred to <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Material_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Force Material Command">Air Force Material Command</a> for use as a radar test bed aircraft and was redesignated as an NT-43A. The T-43 was retired by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Education_and_Training_Command" title="Air Education and Training Command">Air Education and Training Command</a> in 2010 after 37 years of service.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="C-40_Clipper">C-40 Clipper</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: C-40 Clipper">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_100126-N-0705K-003_Naval_Air_Crewmen_2nd_Class_Nicole_Garza_and_David_Murray_and_Naval_Air_Crewman_1st_Class_David_McDermott_load_cargo_bound_for_Haiti_at_Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/US_Navy_100126-N-0705K-003_Naval_Air_Crewmen_2nd_Class_Nicole_Garza_and_David_Murray_and_Naval_Air_Crewman_1st_Class_David_McDermott_load_cargo_bound_for_Haiti_at_Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/US_Navy_100126-N-0705K-003_Naval_Air_Crewmen_2nd_Class_Nicole_Garza_and_David_Murray_and_Naval_Air_Crewman_1st_Class_David_McDermott_load_cargo_bound_for_Haiti_at_Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/US_Navy_100126-N-0705K-003_Naval_Air_Crewmen_2nd_Class_Nicole_Garza_and_David_Murray_and_Naval_Air_Crewman_1st_Class_David_McDermott_load_cargo_bound_for_Haiti_at_Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_100126-N-0705K-003_Naval_Air_Crewmen_2nd_Class_Nicole_Garza_and_David_Murray_and_Naval_Air_Crewman_1st_Class_David_McDermott_load_cargo_bound_for_Haiti_at_Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">Boeing C-40 Clipper</a> is a military version of the 737-700C</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">Boeing C-40 Clipper</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-40_Clipper" title="Boeing C-40 Clipper">Boeing C-40 Clipper</a> is a military version of the 737-700C NG. It is used by both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>, and has been ordered by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fg181205_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fg181205-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Technically, only the Navy C-40A variant is named "Clipper", whereas the USAF C-40B/C variants are officially unnamed.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="P-8_Poseidon">P-8 Poseidon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: P-8 Poseidon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">Boeing P-8 Poseidon</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/170px-US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/255px-US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/340px-US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_P-8_Poseidon_taking_off_at_Perth_Airport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">P-8 Poseidon</a> is a 737-800 variant for <a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare" title="Anti-submarine warfare">anti-submarine warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-surface_warfare" title="Anti-surface warfare">anti-surface warfare</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">shipping interdiction</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Boeing_P-8_Poseidon" title="Boeing P-8 Poseidon">P-8 Poseidon</a> (formerly <i>Multimission Maritime Aircraft</i>) developed for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> by <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Defense,_Space_%26_Security" title="Boeing Defense, Space &amp; Security">Boeing Defense, Space &amp; Security</a>, based on the Next Generation 737-800ERX.  The P-8 can be operated in the <a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare" title="Anti-submarine warfare">anti-submarine warfare</a> (ASW), <a href="/wiki/Anti-surface_warfare" title="Anti-surface warfare">anti-surface warfare</a> (ASUW), and <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">shipping interdiction</a> roles. It is armed with <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpoon_(missile)" title="Harpoon (missile)">Harpoon anti-ship missiles</a> and other weapons, and is able to drop and monitor <a href="/wiki/Sonobuoy" title="Sonobuoy">sonobuoys</a>, as well as operate in conjunction with other assets such as the <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_MQ-4C_Triton" title="Northrop Grumman MQ-4C Triton">Northrop Grumman MQ-4C Triton</a> maritime surveillance <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicle</a> (UAV).\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Boeing_Business_Jet_.28BBJ.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boeing_Business_Jet_(BBJ)">Boeing Business Jet (BBJ)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Boeing Business Jet (BBJ)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Business_Jet" title="Boeing Business Jet">Boeing Business Jet</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/170px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/255px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg/340px-Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="682" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-75V_BBJ_AN1425895.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>BBJ cabin example</div></div></div>\n<p>In the late 1980s, Boeing marketed the 77-33 jet, a business jet version of the 737-300.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The name was short-lived. After the introduction of the Next Generation series, Boeing introduced the Boeing Business Jet (BBJ) series. The BBJ1 was similar in dimensions to the 737-700 but had additional features, including stronger wings and landing gear from the 737-800, and had increased range over the other 737 models through the use of extra fuel tanks. The first BBJ rolled out on August 11, 1998, and flew for the first time on September 4.<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On October 11, 1999, Boeing launched the BBJ2. Based on the 737-800, it is 19&#160;feet 2&#160;inches (5.84&#160;m) longer than the BBJ, with 25% more cabin space and twice the baggage space, but has slightly reduced range. It is also fitted with auxiliary belly fuel tanks and winglets. The first BBJ2 was delivered on February 28, 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-BBJ_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBJ-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing\'s BBJ3 is based on the 737-900ER. The BBJ3 has 1,120 square feet (104&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of floor space, 35% more interior space, and 89% more luggage space than the BBJ2. It has an auxiliary fuel system, giving it a range of up to 4,725 nautical miles (8,751&#160;km), and a <a href="/wiki/Head-up_display" title="Head-up display">Head-up display</a>. Boeing completed the first example in August 2008. This aircraft\'s cabin is pressurized to a simulated 6,500-foot (2,000&#160;m) altitude.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boeing_Converted_Freighter_program">Boeing Converted Freighter program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Boeing Converted Freighter program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Boeing Converted Freighter program (BCF), or the 737-800BCF program, was launched by Boeing in 2016. It converts old 737-800 passenger jets to dedicated freighters.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The first 737-800BCF was delivered in 2018 to GECAS, which is leased to <a href="/wiki/West_Atlantic_UK" title="West Atlantic UK">West Atlantic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Boeing has signed an agreement with Chinese YTO Cargo Airlines to provide the airline with 737-800BCFs pending a planned program launch.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Competition_between_Airbus_and_Boeing#Airbus_A320_vs_Boeing_737" title="Competition between Airbus and Boeing">Competition between Airbus A320 and Boeing 737</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg/220px-United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg/330px-United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg/440px-United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1046" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Airlines_Airbus_A320_and_Boeing_737-800_on_final_approach_at_San_Francisco.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United Airlines Airbus A320 (front) and Boeing 737-900 on final approach</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png/220px-737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png/330px-737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png/440px-737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="626" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:737_vs_a320_family_deliveries_per_model_1967-2018.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 vs A320 family deliveries per model 1967-2018</div></div></div>\n<p>The Boeing 737 Classic, Next Generation and MAX series have faced significant competition from the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320_family" title="Airbus A320 family">Airbus A320 family</a> first introduced in 1988. The relatively recent <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A220" title="Airbus A220">Airbus A220 family</a> now also competes against the smaller capacity end of the 737 variants. The A320 was developed to compete also with the McDonnell Douglas MD-80/90 and 95 series; the 95 later becoming the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_717" title="Boeing 717">Boeing 717</a>. Since July 2017, Airbus had a 59.4% market share of the re-engined single aisle market, while Boeing had 40.6%; Boeing had doubts on over-ordered A320neos by new operators and expected to narrow the gap with replacements not already ordered.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> However, in July 2017, Airbus had still 1,350 more A320neo orders than Boeing had for the 737 MAX.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing delivered 8,918 of the 737 family between March 1988 and December 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a> delivered 8,605 A320 family aircraft over a similar period since first delivery in early 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_Orders_2_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_Orders_2-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1017906329">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div></dd></dl>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian">Civilian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Civilian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_737_operators" title="List of Boeing 737 operators">List of Boeing 737 operators</a></div>\n<p>The 737 is operated by more than 500 airlines, flying to 1,200 destinations in 190 countries: over 4,500 are in service and at any given time there are on average 1,250 airborne worldwide. On average, somewhere in the world, a 737 took off or landed every five seconds in 2006. Since entering service in 1968, the 737 has carried over 12 billion passengers over 74 billion miles (120 billion km; 65 billion nm), and has accumulated more than 296 million hours in the air. The 737 represents more than 25% of the worldwide fleet of large commercial jet airliners.<sup id="cite_ref-flight7feb2006_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight7feb2006-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-737facts_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737facts-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Many countries operate the 737 passenger, BBJ, and cargo variants in government or military applications.<sup id="cite_ref-militaryuse_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-militaryuse-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Users with 737s include:\n</p>\n<style title="Turkey">Turkey</a></li>\n<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orders_and_deliveries">Orders and deliveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Orders and deliveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png/220px-737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png/330px-737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png/440px-737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png 2x" data-file-width="993" data-file-height="615" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:737-deliveries-per-year-1967-2018.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 deliveries per year, 1967-2018</div></div></div>\n<p>Boeing delivered the 5,000th 737 to <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> on February 13, 2006, the 6,000th 737 to <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Air_Shuttle" title="Norwegian Air Shuttle">Norwegian Air Shuttle</a> in April 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-Flight22apr2009_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22apr2009-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> the 8,000th 737 to <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> on April 16, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The 10,000th 737 was ordered in July 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and rolled out on March 13, 2018, as over 4,600 orders were pending.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight13march2018_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight13march2018-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 2006, there were an average of 1,250 Boeing 737s airborne at any given time, with two either departing or landing somewhere every five seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-flight7feb2006_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight7feb2006-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\nThe 737 was the most commonly flown aircraft in 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> and 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, there were  6,512 737 airliners in service (5,567 <a href="/wiki/737NG" class="mw-redirect" title="737NG">737NGs</a> plus 945 737-200s and <a href="/wiki/737_Classic" class="mw-redirect" title="737 Classic">737 Classics</a>), more than the 6,510 A320 family.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, there were  6,858 737 airliners in service (5,968 737NGs plus 890 737-200s and classics), less than the 6,965 A320 family.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (March 2020)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The 737 had the highest, cumulative orders for any airliner until surpassed by the A320 family in October 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight15nov2019_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight15nov2019-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> As of March&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 14,690 units of the Boeing 737 family had been ordered, with 4,013 units to be delivered, or 3,240 when including "additional criteria for recognizing contracted backlog with customers beyond the existence of a firm contract" (ASC 606 Adjustment).<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: right; font-size: 96%;">\n<caption>Deliveries by year for all 737 generations and model series<sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-8" class="reference"><a class="wikitable" style="text-align: right; font-size: B3XM</td>\n<td>4,437</td>\n<td>472</td>\n<td>3,965</td>\n<td>January 29, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 2019, 737 orders dropped by 90%, as 737 MAX orders dried up after the March grounding.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="A specialized aviation media ref would be more relevant than a general tech blog (January 2020)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The 737 MAX backlog fell by 182, mainly due to the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Airways" title="Jet Airways">Jet Airways</a> bankruptcy, a drop in Boeing\'s airliner backlog was a first in at least the past 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">Accidents and incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Accidents and incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_accidents_and_incidents_involving_the_Boeing_737" title="List of accidents and incidents involving the Boeing 737">List of accidents and incidents involving the Boeing 737</a></div>\n<p>As of January&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, there has been a total of 502 <a href="/wiki/Aviation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Aviation accidents and incidents">aviation accidents and incidents</a> involving all 737 aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> including 218 <a href="/wiki/Hull_loss" title="Hull loss">hull losses</a> resulting in a total of 5,585 fatalities.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A Boeing analysis of commercial jet airplane accidents between 1959 and 2013 found that the hull loss rate for the Original series was 1.75 per million departures, for the Classic series 0.54, and the Next Generation series 0.27.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1990s, a series of <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_rudder_issues" title="Boeing 737 rudder issues">rudder issues</a> on series -200 and -300 aircraft resulted in multiple incidents. In two total loss accidents, <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines_Flight_585" title="United Airlines Flight 585">United Airlines Flight 585</a> (a -200 series) and <a href="/wiki/USAir_Flight_427" title="USAir Flight 427">USAir Flight 427</a>, (a -300), the pilots lost control of the aircraft following a sudden and unexpected deflection of the <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a>, killing everyone aboard, a total of 157 people.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Similar rudder issues led to a temporary loss of control on at least five other 737 flights before the problem was ultimately identified. The <a href="/wiki/National_Transportation_Safety_Board" title="National Transportation Safety Board">National Transportation Safety Board</a> determined that the accidents and incidents were the result of a design flaw that could result in an uncommanded movement of the aircraft\'s rudder.<sup id="cite_ref-AAR01-01_(UA_Flight_585_Amended)_Final_Report_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAR01-01_(UA_Flight_585_Amended)_Final_Report-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>13</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NTSB_AAR-99-01_(USAir_Flight_427)_Final_Report_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTSB_AAR-99-01_(USAir_Flight_427)_Final_Report-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>ix</span></sup> As a result of the NTSB\'s findings, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> ordered that the rudder servo valves be replaced on all 737s and mandated new training protocols for pilots to handle an unexpected movement of control surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the crashes of two 737 MAX 8 aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air_Flight_610" title="Lion Air Flight 610">Lion Air Flight 610</a> in October 2018 and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Airlines_Flight_302" title="Ethiopian Airlines Flight 302">Ethiopian Airlines Flight 302</a> in March 2019, which caused 346 deaths, <a href="/wiki/National_aviation_authority" title="National aviation authority">national aviation authorities</a> around the world <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Boeing 737 MAX groundings">grounded the 737 MAX series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-maxgroundings_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-maxgroundings-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\nOn December 16, 2019, Boeing announced that it would suspend production of the 737 MAX from January 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing16dec2019_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing16dec2019-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Production of the MAX series resumed on May 27, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_on_display">Aircraft on display</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Aircraft on display">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tukwila,_WA,_USA_-_panoramio_(11).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg/220px-Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg/330px-Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg/440px-Tukwila%2C_WA%2C_USA_-_panoramio_%2811%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4128" data-file-height="2322" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tukwila,_WA,_USA_-_panoramio_(11).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USAir" class="mw-redirect" title="USAir">USAir</a> 737-200 fuselage section at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Flight</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Owing to the 737\'s long production history and popularity, many older 737s have found use in museums after reaching the end of useful service.\n</p>\n<ul><li>19437/1: 737-130 registered N515NA on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Flight</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="Seattle, Washington">Seattle, Washington</a>. It was the first 737 built and is painted in NASA markings.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>19047/14: 737-222 registered N9009U preserved by <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Carbondale" title="Southern Illinois University Carbondale">Southern Illinois University Carbondale</a> at <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_Airport" title="Southern Illinois Airport">Southern Illinois Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>20213/160: 737-201 registered N213US forward fuselage on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">Museum of Flight</a> in Seattle, Washington, in <a href="/wiki/USAir" class="mw-redirect" title="USAir">USAir</a> livery.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>20561/292: 737-281 registered LV-WTX on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Aeron%C3%A1utica_de_Argentina" title="Museo Nacional de Aeron\xc3\xa1utica de Argentina">National Museum of Aeronautics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mor%C3%B3n,_Buenos_Aires" title="Mor\xc3\xb3n, Buenos Aires">Mor\xc3\xb3n, Buenos Aires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>20562/293: 737-281 registered CC-CSK fuselage preserved at Motel Bah\xc3\xada in <a href="/wiki/Conc%C3%B3n" title="Conc\xc3\xb3n">Conc\xc3\xb3n</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>21262/470: 737-2H4 registered C-GWJT on static display at the <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia_Institute_of_Technology" title="British Columbia Institute of Technology">British Columbia Institute of Technology</a> Aerospace Technology Campus in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_British_Columbia" title="Richmond, British Columbia">Richmond, British Columbia</a>. It is used for ground instructional training. The aircraft was donated by <a href="/wiki/WestJet" title="WestJet">WestJet</a> and bears its livery.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>21340/499: 737-2H4 registered N29SW on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Aviation_Museum" title="Kansas Aviation Museum">Kansas Aviation Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>. It was formerly operated by <a href="/wiki/Ryan_International_Airlines" title="Ryan International Airlines">Ryan International Airlines</a> and prior to that <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>21712/557: 737-275 registered C-GIPW preserved in operational condition at <a href="/wiki/Alberta_Flying_Heritage_Museum" title="Alberta Flying Heritage Museum">Alberta Flying Heritage Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Villeneuve,_Alberta" title="Villeneuve, Alberta">Villeneuve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>. Painted in <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Western_Airlines" title="Pacific Western Airlines">Pacific Western Airlines</a> livery.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>22578/767: 737-290C registered N740AS on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Aviation_Heritage_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Alaska Aviation Heritage Museum">Alaska Aviation Heritage Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anchorage,_Alaska" title="Anchorage, Alaska">Anchorage, Alaska</a>. It was formerly operated by <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>22826/878: 737-2H4 registered YV1361 preserved at a hotel in <a href="/wiki/Santiago" title="Santiago">Santiago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>. It was formerly operated by <a href="/wiki/Avior_Airlines" title="Avior Airlines">Avior Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>23059/980: 737-2Z6 registered 22\xe2\x80\x93222 on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Thai_Air_Force_Museum" title="Royal Thai Air Force Museum">Royal Thai Air Force Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>22940/1037: 737-3H4 registered N300SW on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Frontiers_of_Flight_Museum" title="Frontiers of Flight Museum">Frontiers of Flight Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas, Texas</a>. It was the first such aircraft delivered to <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> in November 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>23257/1124: 737-301 registered PK-AWU on static display at <a href="/wiki/ITE_College_Central" title="ITE College Central">ITE College Central</a> in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>23472/1194: 737-219 registered ZS-SMD on static display at the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Airways_Museum_Society" title="South African Airways Museum Society">South African Airways Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germiston,_Gauteng" class="mw-redirect" title="Germiston, Gauteng">Germiston, Gauteng</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>23660/1294: 737-377 registered G-CELS (nickname Elsie) on static display at Norwich International Aviation Academy, as an aircraft maintenance trainer. It is painted in the silver &amp; red <a href="/wiki/Jet2.com" title="Jet2.com">Jet2.com</a> colour scheme, without the logo branding.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>27286/2528: 737-3Q8 registered N759BA on static display at the <a href="/wiki/Pima_Air_%26_Space_Museum" title="Pima Air &amp; Space Museum">Pima Air &amp; Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tucson,_Arizona" title="Tucson, Arizona">Tucson, Arizona</a>. It is painted in <a href="/wiki/China_Southern_Airlines" title="China Southern Airlines">China Southern Airlines</a> markings, and was previously operated by the airline as B-2921.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span
